### Post by Zakhafr on 2008-09-04
> **Kezu said:**
> Dell refers to it as "Mini OS" Ubuntu.  Is there going to be anything worthwhile different about it compared with a Ubuntu install that I could do with downloading the desktop version 8.04?  

I like how if you max out the options, if is cheaper to get a Windows XP version...

Yes indeed... well I hope so !

See the Ubuntu site. There is what they call a "Netbook remix".

And one of the main goals of Intrepid is to adapt Ubuntu to run smoothly on this kind of machines.
Important things are:
- prevent the system from exhausting the SSD with to many writes. Because a SSD is not a hard disk, and it has a much more limited number of writes to the same memory cell. An example of modification could be to switch down to ext2 filesystem instead of ext3 who logs a lot.
- check dialog box so that they fit in the small screens or can be sized. Try to switch your screen to 800x600, and you'll understand :)
Some application are just a pain to use. Even system configuration tools.
- tune the system to remove unnecessary software so that it takes less disk space. You can still download additional software if you wish to (through the repositories) but the "core" needs to be as small as possible... especially when the Ubuntu basic version comes with a 4GB "disk"!
I have a Mandriva USB version that runs fine on a 2GB USB stick, and I still have 0,75GB left for data, once everything is up and running (even LAMP server for example!)


So yes definitely, before Intrepid is out, I do hope the version Dell will ship is not the "regular" desktop version, but an adapted remix.

---

### Post by Talon2 on 2008-09-04
> **mikewhatever said:**
> Well, if you go for max specs, the one with XP costs 534-55=479, and with Ubuntu, 494, 15$ more expensive. I don't expect Dell to leave out all those wanting XP, but this kind of pricing is ridiculous.

There is nothing ridiculous about this pricing.  There are two reasons why:

1.  Manufacturers such as Dell sell advertising on the desktops of the computers they sell.  It is called Bloatware.  There is a big demand to place bloatware on Windows pre-loaded systems.  There is no demand to place bloatware on Linux systems at this point.

2.  Manufacturers  have an economy of scale going when it comes to supporting Windows based systems.  No such economy of scale is present for Linux based systems on the desktop as market share is simply too low.

What can done to change the economics of the situation?  Buy Linux (Ubuntu) pre-loaded systems and convince others to do the same.  Increased market share will fix this problem.  When I see someone who is an Ubuntu user come on here and say something like "I bought the Vista pre-loaded version since it was $10 cheaper then I wiped Vista and loaded Ubuntu it makes me cringe because Microsoft wins when that happens.

---

### Post by mikewhatever on 2008-09-04
> **Talon2 said:**
> What can done to change the economics of the situation?  Buy Linux (Ubuntu) pre-loaded systems and convince others to do the same.  Increased market share will fix this problem.  When I see someone who is an Ubuntu user come on here and say something like "I bought the Vista pre-loaded version since it was $10 cheaper then I wiped Vista and loaded Ubuntu it makes me cringe because Microsoft wins when that happens.

This is unreasonable. Given the much smaller market share of Ubuntu over Windows, there is simply not enough users to convince others. There must be a way to make Ubuntu powered machines cheaper then Windows powered ones, thus providing an initial economical advantage. Without that, XP on netbooks is going to be very difficult to compete with.

---

### Post by anjilslaire on 2008-09-06
[http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578]("http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578")

> 
In terms of noise, our pre-production Mini 9 is quite remarkable in that it makes literally no noise. There are no cooling fans or spinning hard drives to bother you (or the people seated next to you) while you work. Of course, the fact that the Mini 9 lacks a cooling fan is likely why the temperatures on this netbook are slightly higher than what we've seen on some other Atom-based netbooks. Still, the Mini 9 never becomes unacceptably warm, so we accept this minor heat increase in exchange for absolute silence.

### Post by abeisgreat on 2008-09-08
I ordered a Mini 9 with xp on it, hoping of course to install a full weight linux distro because the "netbook remix" looks unappealing to me, hopefully around the time dell releases the machine someone will also release the specific netbook remix iso so I can try that as well.

### Post by superm1 on 2008-09-08
> **abeisgreat said:**
> I ordered a Mini 9 with xp on it, hoping of course to install a full weight linux distro because the "netbook remix" looks unappealing to me, hopefully around the time dell releases the machine someone will also release the specific netbook remix iso so I can try that as well.

what do you think the chances of being able to get the "Mini OS" somewhere online will be? :???:
Please keep in mind a few things.  The Canonical "netbook remix" is not what is on these devices.  There is a custom UI that was developed for them that comes up.  If you don't want to use it, there is an option in the Ubuntu menu to switch back to the normal Ubuntu UI.

You can see it a little bit here: [http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578](http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578)

Also, standard Ubuntu 8.04 will not run perfectly on these devices.  A lot of customization has been put into place that ties the Ubuntu install that's shipped with them to the exact device.  Things such as controlling Bluetooth and WLAN, being built for the atom's architecture (LPIA), and a lot of applications had to have their UI modified to be able to fit on such a small resolution.  Some of this customization will start to show up in Ubuntu 8.10, some won't .  Some of it is just wed very specifically to this hardware.

Also, like Dell laptop & desktop machines, if you purchase this with Linux preloaded, you will be receiving a codec package that adds support for a handful of codecs that don't normally work well.

All in all, at least for now, if you are planning to use a Mini 9 with Linux, *buy* it with Linux.

### Post by sloggerkhan on 2008-09-08
> **RichTJ99 said:**
> How is that Atom processor in terms of speed?  Is it comparable to a 1.6 C2D (if there is such a speed)?  Or is this close to a pentium M?

There are cpu reviews all over. It's much slower than a C2D, though evidently hyper threading apps really helps performance.

Most reviews show via nano to kick its but, and if there were current models of netbook running that nano because of nano's improved chipset efficiency making up for its somewhat higher power draw, nano would probably be an overall better option. However, as atom's chipset improves, it should scale into a level where you have nano at very low power consumption, but less power than nano, nano with more power draw and better performance, then a bit of a gap, then mainstream processors. 

So far as pentium M, I don't know how that works it, though the nano is certainly much closer to a pentium m than a c2d.

---

### Post by Talon2 on 2008-09-08
> **superm1 said:**
> If you are planning to use a Mini 9 with Linux, *buy* it with Linux.

This is very good advice.

I've been reading this forum for over a year and it surprises me how many Ubuntu users buy Dell notebook systems with XP or Vista.  The reasons seem to vary but the result is the same:

If you buy a system with a Microsoft operating system pre-loaded you do three things:

1.  You send money to Microsoft (and Microsoft is not our friend).

2.  You send a message to Dell (or other computer maker) that Windows is what you want.

3.  You get a system that may contain Linux hostile hardware.  Dell limits the hardware on the Ubuntu pre-loaded systems to hardware that is Linux friendly.

Find a way to buy Ubuntu pre-loaded systems.

---

### Post by anjilslaire on 2008-09-09
> **superm1 said:**
> Please keep in mind a few things.  The Canonical "netbook remix" is not what is on these devices.  There is a custom UI that was developed for them that comes up.  If you don't want to use it, there is an option in the Ubuntu menu to switch back to the normal Ubuntu UI.

You can see it a little bit here: [http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578](http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578)

Also, standard Ubuntu 8.04 will not run perfectly on these devices.  A lot of customization has been put into place that ties the Ubuntu install that's shipped with them to the exact device.  Things such as controlling Bluetooth and WLAN, being built for the atom's architecture (LPIA), and a lot of applications had to have their UI modified to be able to fit on such a small resolution.  Some of this customization will start to show up in Ubuntu 8.10, some won't .  Some of it is just wed very specifically to this hardware.

Also, like Dell laptop & desktop machines, if you purchase this with Linux preloaded, you will be receiving a codec package that adds support for a handful of codecs that don't normally work well.

All in all, at least for now, if you are planning to use a Mini 9 with Linux, *buy* it with Linux.

See above. 8.10 will likely have issues, and 8.04 isn't whats on these things.

---

### Post by jespdj on 2008-09-10
> **anjilslaire said:**
> My concern is, is will we be able to get Ubuntu Netbook Remix individually from Canonical in order to install? According to them, it's only available to OEMs

[http://blog.laptopmag.com/ubuntu-netbook-remix-qa-with-canonical](http://blog.laptopmag.com/ubuntu-netbook-remix-qa-with-canonical)

Is there going to be a netbook install source we can load via usb or something? I want to be able to load a vanilla OS without any Dell labeling, for example.
Where do you read that it will only available to OEMs? Quote from that article:

> **You&#8217;ve mentioned that Ubuntu Netbook Remix isn&#8217;t for the end user; can a person actually download it?**
We traditionally make an ISO available that can be burned to CD. But there&#8217;s been some confusion about the Netbook Remix being made the same way. It won&#8217;t be. If you are an experienced Linux user you can go to our PPA&#8212;what we call our personal product archives. They&#8217;re basically storage repositories. There you&#8217;ll find the launcher. If you know what you&#8217;re doing and are willing to do things that we don&#8217;t recommend, you can get that PPA and put that on top of your desktop image and make it work. We make it available, but it is absolutely not recommended for someone with a Eee PC who, say, decides to get the Netbook Remix and try to make it work themselves. It can be done, but it&#8217;s not recommended for the standard user.
In other words: You can [get it here](https://launchpad.net/netbook-remix) and "it can be done, but it&#8217;s not recommended for the standard user." They don't provide an easy-to-use ISO, but it's ofcourse not kept hidden or secret or made available only to OEMs. Because of the open source licenses such as the GPL, Canonical would not even be allowed to do that.

---

### Post by anjilslaire on 2008-09-10
Yes, its *possible* to get it, but its intended for OEMs, and they are not releasing a public ISO, to install from scratch.
[http://www.canonical.com/netbooks](http://www.canonical.com/netbooks)
> 
To use the Ubuntu Netbook Remix you need to install packages on top of an existing Ubuntu installation and you may have some compatibility issues depending on your hardware profile. For now we recommend it only for experienced Linux users or commercial OEMs and ODMs engage with Canonical for support and service offerings.

In addition to the Ubuntu Netbook Remix there will be pre-installed remixes made available on manufacturer's machines. These will contain software that is not free and built for specific hardware profiles unique to the OEM. These will not be publicly available as we do not have the right to redistribute the software. 

I.E. they don't intend it for teh average user. As you noted, it available thruogh the repo's. 
Which means its installed on top of an existing Ubuntu install. 
Which means it's not a clean, netbook-only installation.

That's my only point. Yes, I can shoe-horn it on, but its not elegant, and not what is intended for a netbook in the real world. It'll give you the new new GUI, but not th optimizations & strip-downs for a true netbook.

Hopefully that will be remedied at a later date :)

---

### Post by Zakhafr on 2008-09-10

Anjislaire is right, it's generally not very wise to use an O.S. just after it is released. There are usually quite a lot of unfixed bugs.
That's why I waited for the 8.04**.1**

Dell might migrate to 8.10 later... but who knows... 8.04 has a big advantage, it is a LTS. And as the focus of 8.10 is to be able to run on those small machines and the 8.04 customized by Dell will already have it, IMHO you will not see a big difference.

Now with the previous posts, this litte machine looks more and more exciting (I'm speaking of the Ubuntu version, of course !).

Although I totally disagree with the journalist when he said it is more difficult to install things in Ubuntu than in Windows. When it comes to mainstream application, you just go to the menu "Applications" >> "Add/Delete", choose the application you want... and it's done.

Of course, if you want to Install a LAMP server it's a little bit more difficult (not that much), but then you are a Geek and you do perfectly know what you are doing with the console!

I just wish this computer was not so overpriced in Europe :(

So at the moment I'll still wait for competition to do it's job and pull the prices down.

... and also wait for the Ubuntu version to be available of course, as I'm not at all interested by a Windows version.

### Post by gregconquest on 2008-09-20
> **Tinkerman said:**
> I pre-ordered my Mini 9 a few days ago. The estimated shipping date is 10/10. I don't mind the wait.

I ordered it with Ubuntu, 512K Ram, 8GB SSD and it was $399 total with ***free*** shipping to Hawaii!:-P

My main reason for buying this is simply to play around with the SSD as a recording media. I'm a part-time sound engineer and I'm anxious to see how it performs recording a few tracks via a USB audio interface. I'll be using [Audacity]("http://audacity.sourceforge.net/") as a software solution. I've used it a few other laptops runnuing Ubuntu and it works fairly well.

The big attraction is of course the silent operation. No spinning hard drive, no cooling fans... just silence. 

I'm sure it will be great for travel and mobile office appplications as well.

I have a suggestion for you:
- If the Mini 9 can use SDHC cards OK, then get a 16GB SDHC card and have a lot more solid-state memory for storage / recording (off loading to a USB drive when not recording)

Greg

PS You may be able to install a 1 or 2 GB SoDIMM in the mini as well. The mini uses a 200-pin (notebook size) DDR2 667 (PC5300) SoDIMM. 2GB versions can be purchased for $40 or so US. I have a 2GB SoDIMM ready to put into my system already.

3-in-1 Media card reader - supports:
. Secure Digital (SD) memory card
. Secure Digital High Capacity (SDHC) card
. Multi media Card (MMC)
. Memory Stick

The background looks like it took them 5 minutes to make in photoshop.....or ms paint.  I used to love windows xp but now I know I will never use it again.

Getting the ram upgrade through dell is a waste of money.  You can buy a 2 gig for the price the 1 gig upgrade is online and dell doesn't even offer a 2 gig upgrade.

I found the best thing to do was to buy the xp version and use unetbootin.  I used the ubuntu 8.4 network install which worked perfect over the wired network.  The only thing was the sound didn't work right away, but i fixed that by searching the forums.

### Post by Yfrwlf on 2008-09-28

### Post by brnetonboy on 2008-09-30

---

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-30
> **brnetonboy said:**
> Aside from the issue of dual booting--why is it so important that Ubuntu is preinstalled?
Options.

Right now, Ubuntu is just emerging as a tentative option. If Linux users continue to buy Windows preinstalled and install Linux themselves, they're sending a message (voting with their wallets) to Dell and other OEMs that having a Linux option isn't worth it, and then they'll just keep selling Windows almost exclusively.

---

### Post by brnetonboy on 2008-09-30
> **aysiu said:**
> Options.

Right now, Ubuntu is just emerging as a tentative option. If Linux users continue to buy Windows preinstalled and install Linux themselves, they're sending a message (voting with their wallets) to Dell and other OEMs that having a Linux option isn't worth it, and then they'll just keep selling Windows almost exclusively.

Granted--and I agree. But wouldn't purchasing a Dell Remix installation disc be "voting with your wallet"?

---

### Post by aysiu on 2008-09-30
> **brnetonboy said:**
> Granted--and I agree. But wouldn't purchasing a Dell Remix installation disc be "voting with your wallet"?
Well, they would be voting with their wallets. They'd be voting to pay for a Linux distro to install themselves instead of voting for a Linux distro preinstalled.

Preinstalled is key to wider adoption, as only tech-savvy power users install Linux distros themselves, and many non-tech-savvy users are even afraid to or don't know how to use restore discs.

I'd love to be in a position where people are asking me which computer they should buy--Windows, Mac, or Ubuntu. I don't want to be in a position where people are buying Windows computers and then I have to say "Did you ever consider buying the Ubuntu remix installation disc for that computer?"

---

### Post by Talon2 on 2008-09-30
> **brnetonboy said:**
> Aside from the issue of dual booting--why is it so important that Ubuntu is preinstalled?

I'm glad you asked.

I've been following the small computer industry since before the original IBM PC came to life.  I've used CPM, MS-DOS 2.11, PC DOS 3, DR-DOS 5 and 6, OS/2 v1-4, eCS and Ubuntu over the years.  I have refused to use Microsoft products since MS-DOS 2.11 as I recognized that Microsoft was using illegal business practices.  Such practices continue to this day and have made it extremely difficult for any competing operating system to gain enough traction to make it economical for developers to support the platform.  It takes money to invest in a platform and the best evidence that a platform is being used is the sales data.  Preload data is solid data whereas downloaded iso's data means very little.  I certainly wouldn't invest money based on downloaded iso data.  Whether a company such as Dell continues to provide preloaded Ubuntu systems depends entirely on whether they can make money from doing so.  Dell and other computers makers look at hard data when they make decisions.  They know what they have sold.  If you buy an XP preloaded system then they know that.  If you then wipe XP and load Ubuntu they won't know that so it can't figure into their future plans.  Low numbers of sold Ubuntu preloads = no Ubuntu preloads in the future.

If you support Microsoft then you need to buy the XP version but if you support Ubuntu then you need to but the Ubuntu version.

---

Greg

PS And be practical too. Encouraging everyone here to get a system preloaded with ubuntu should be our goal. Sharing linux is just another one of the freedoms of open source.

I'm not accusing anyone, that is what would happen if Dell's licensed codecs were to be shared out: It is wrong. The codecs in question are not free open source.

Just because you personally have a license for them from your Japanese installation doesn't make it OK & legal to share them out in another (English) image for others. 

And BTW, it isn't only your name I've seen pop up around here suggesting this.
Others are actually discussing imaging their base Ubuntu drive (not recovery image) & sharing it out with others who bought the Windows version and want to load Ubuntu on it. *That* is wrong.

Bottom line: If you want this thing with all the extra perks included in the Ubuntu version (codecs, custom GUI, optimizations, etc) **BUY IT with Ubuntu loaded,** don't get Windows and expect someone to give you the Dell version if Dell hasn't released it.

Now Greg, I understand your case is a bit different, as you have a non-English version version of Ubuntu, so much of this may not apply in your case, but that is what you get when you order something (Japanese version) and want something different (English version).

If Dell releases a public image, then we'll all be happy.

---

### Post by thejoefish on 2008-10-02
This has been my experience with the Inspiron Mini -

For full disclosure I work for Dell server support.  I'm not a Dell fanboy...they make some good products, have good enterprise support and crappy consumer support.  I'd change a lot of things about the company.

I started wanting a mini 9 when it was leaked way back.  The 15% discount on these guys made them really nice.

I ordered mine the day it came out, September 4th I think.  I saw that the Ubuntu ones were all preorder, so I skipped it in favor of XP.  I ordered it with 1 gig ram, no bluetooth, 16 gig ssd.

The initial ship date was September 19th.  I tracked it every day on our internal system and saw virtually the same thing a lot of you have seen...long in production time.  At Dell that's basically anytime after they've verified payment and before it's shipped, so that state means nothing.  I got a message on the 19th saying it had been delayed.  If you get that on your date it should ship that's an automated message that is mandated by a postal act.  They have to ask you if you still want the order at that point...so don't panic.  Mine actually shipped the same day.  I think it's no an Ubuntu issue at this point.  It took quite a while to get the Xp ones ready.  I've Dell laptops ship the next day before.  I think they actually just announced this before they had all the parts ready...largely because of pressure to get it out on the ideastorm blog.

So I got it...I actually thought the packaging was okay.  It seemed pretty protected to me.  Plus it was much "greener" packaging than I've seen before.

The keyboard itself does take getting used to...but really I don't use the F keys very much.  I have had it hooked up right next to my desktop and use synergy while at home on it, and it works well.

XP was actually fairly speedy after all the Dell proprietary BS was gone.  At first I had backtrack running inside of virtualbox (yes, it works just fine) until I realized that kismet would not run off of a vm adapter.  Noob mistake.  It functioned well with two vm's open.  I wasn't sure how the atom would do there, but seems okay.  XP's battery life was low though...right at two hours.  It did actually run world of warcraft just fine at the lowest setting...over my home wifi.  Took about two minutes to load but after that ran just fine.  I'll test it under wine over wifi later.

I installed ubuntu via wubi and verified that the BC43 cutter worked with the BC 4312.  At that point I did a liveusb install of ubuntu 8.04.  Battery life is actually very good imo...at least for a three cell battery.  It's right at 4:15 from a full charge.  I saw some other people complaining...but it's a three cell battery.  I doubt I'll ever use this for more than 4 hours a day away from a plug.  That's using wifi and xp under virtualbox.  (As an aside, why does all it training seem to work only under windows?  Wine rarely works.  Even linux training!)

The one thing I have had trouble with is hibernate.  I get a kernel panic when using it.  I haven't really researched it though...maybe an easy fix.  Suspend works just fine.

I'm not sure what the Dell remix is really going to look like.  Most everything worked out of the box.  The power issue is probably nothing and installing the broadcom drivers was very simple.  Wireless may have worked out of the box, but I didn't test it because I needed monitor mode support.

Anyway...long winded...but this a good machine, good for linux.  Couple of questions for anyone who'd care to answer.

What do you guys think of the ext3 vs ext2 and swap issue on these?  The manufacturers seem to say that write balancing will lead to about 15 years or so under even heavy writes.

Also...the netbook remix doesn't look that great to me.  I like the base desktop.  Is there anything there that's a performance enhancement?  It looks mostly to be replacement window managers, etc.

Joefish

---

### Post by emptythevoid on 2008-10-13
It does seem a little odd that Dell wouldn't have the netbook remix, since the mini 9 has the atom processor and the remix is supposed to make better use of the atom chipset.  Maybe the remix wasn't available or stable enough by the time Dell began development of the Mini 9.

On the plus side, though, it could mean that if you needed to re-install Ubuntu, all you'd need as a bootable USB key with the regular Ubuntu image (since the Dell Ubuntu customization has some non-free software, you cannot get the custom image from Canonical).  At most, you wouldn't have the Dell bar, and any custom software (codecs) Dell pre-installed on the machine.  But perhaps those would be available as downloads from Dell's support site.  (I don't know how the codec licensing deal works, so forgive me if I'm being naive here.)

### Post by wolfkeeper on 2008-10-13
That's not what was stated earlier in this thread. Go back to page 5...

> Please keep in mind a few things. The Canonical "netbook remix" is not what is on these devices. There is a custom UI that was developed for them that comes up. If you don't want to use it, there is an option in the Ubuntu menu to switch back to the normal Ubuntu UI.

You can see it a little bit here: [http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578](http://www.notebookreview.com/default.asp?newsID=4578)

Also, standard Ubuntu 8.04 will not run perfectly on these devices. A lot of customization has been put into place that ties the Ubuntu install that's shipped with them to the exact device. Things such as controlling Bluetooth and WLAN, being built for the atom's architecture (LPIA), and a lot of applications had to have their UI modified to be able to fit on such a small resolution. Some of this customization will start to show up in Ubuntu 8.10, some won't . Some of it is just wed very specifically to this hardware.

Also, like Dell laptop & desktop machines, if you purchase this with Linux preloaded, you will be receiving a codec package that adds support for a handful of codecs that don't normally work well.

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-14
Here's how I fixed my too-small partition:

*) Open up a terminal.  It's under "accessories" in the Ubuntu menu.
*) Type "sudo fdisk /dev/sda".  You will be asked for your password.  This opens up fdisk, which is a disk partition editor.  
*) Hit "p" to print out your current partition scheme.  Write down the start number for the partition /dev/sda2.  This number is important.  Mine was 48196.  Yours might be, too.
*) Now you'll delete your main linux partition.  Don't worry.  It doesn't touch your data.  Hit the "d" key and select partition 2.
*) Create a new partition using the "n" key.  This will be a "primary" partition, and will be number 2.  Enter the start sector you wrote down earlier.  For the end sector, just hit enter, and it will default to the largest size it can manage.
*) Use the "a" command to toggle the bootable bit on your new partition.
*) Hit the "p" key again and make sure that your new partition exists and is considered to be a "Linux" partition, and that it has the asterisk that indicates that it is bootable.
*) If you're confident that you got all of that done correctly, use the "w" command to write your changes to the hard drive.  Otherwise, use "q" to exit without making changes.

*) Since you just changed the partition table on your root drive, you'll need to reboot for the changes to be seen by the kernel.  Do that now.  If it boots, you did it right.  :)
*) Find your way back to a terminal, and type "sudo resize2fs /dev/sda2".  You will be asked for your password again.  Since we didn't supply a size, it will resize the filesystem to match the partition size. 
*) You're done.  Type "df -h" to see how much space you have.  You don't technically need to reboot, but I recommend doing so anyway so it can run a quick boot-time check of the filesystem and make sure everything is OK.  

Feel free to ask questions or point out stupid mistakes.  I can take it.

---

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-14
It's lpia, which stands for "low power on intel architecture" I believe.

[http://lwn.net/Articles/247003/](http://lwn.net/Articles/247003/)

### Post by yelsn on 2008-10-14
> **mfm3 said:**
> Interesting...does this mean we can't install certain programs that don't have lpia packages?

[https://bugs.launchpad.net/netbook-remix/+bug/262890](https://bugs.launchpad.net/netbook-remix/+bug/262890)

Well I just got Opera installed by using the first suggestion in that bug report.   After downloading the i386 Opera for 8.04 I opened the terminal moved to the directory where opera's file was and ran:
```
sudo dpkg --force-architecture -i opera_9.60.2444.gcc4.qt3_i386.deb
```

Just replace the filename with whatever you're trying to install.

Also if anyone is trying to sign up for the 2GB from box.net make sure you sign up from [http://www.box.net/dell](http://www.box.net/dell) instead of using the link on the mini's quick launcher (it'll only let you sign up for their regular services).

---

Of course, I decided to use my preferred tools to do the same thing.

I had installed Damn Small Linux to a 256MB USB stick and made it bootable in the BIOS by putting USB storage as the first choice.

A cold reboot, removing the USB stick, and I do indeed have 3.0GB used and 11.3GB free per gparted.

Dell support is flat out wrong that it is a Linux/Ubuntu problem. The system is being installed to a small partition leaving any extra space "unused".

### Post by wolfkeeper on 2008-10-15
> **wolfkeeper said:**
> Anyone know why most applications/windows open maximized, and how to stop that?

I don't need my terminal window to open full screen. And I'd like to keep VLC down to the size of the video...

Found the answer to my own question. There's a little program called "maximus" running the in the background that maximizes new windows as they open. You can add applications to the ignore list via gconf-editor, then going to apps > maximus and editing the exclude-class list.

### Post by theacoustician on 2008-10-15
> **trebonius said:**
> Here's how I fixed my too-small partition:

*) Open up a terminal.  It's under "accessories" in the Ubuntu menu.
*) Type "sudo fdisk /dev/sda".  You will be asked for your password.  This opens up fdisk, which is a disk partition editor.  
*) Hit "p" to print out your current partition scheme.  Write down the start number for the partition /dev/sda2.  This number is important.  Mine was 48196.  Yours might be, too.
*) Now you'll delete your main linux partition.  Don't worry.  It doesn't touch your data.  Hit the "d" key and select partition 2.
*) Create a new partition using the "n" key.  This will be a "primary" partition, and will be number 2.  Enter the start sector you wrote down earlier.  For the end sector, just hit enter, and it will default to the largest size it can manage.
*) Use the "a" command to toggle the bootable bit on your new partition.
*) Hit the "p" key again and make sure that your new partition exists and is considered to be a "Linux" partition, and that it has the asterisk that indicates that it is bootable.
*) If you're confident that you got all of that done correctly, use the "w" command to write your changes to the hard drive.  Otherwise, use "q" to exit without making changes.

*) Since you just changed the partition table on your root drive, you'll need to reboot for the changes to be seen by the kernel.  Do that now.  If it boots, you did it right.  :)
*) Find your way back to a terminal, and type "sudo resize2fs /dev/sda2".  You will be asked for your password again.  Since we didn't supply a size, it will resize the filesystem to match the partition size. 
*) You're done.  Type "df -h" to see how much space you have.  You don't technically need to reboot, but I recommend doing so anyway so it can run a quick boot-time check of the filesystem and make sure everything is OK.  

Feel free to ask questions or point out stupid mistakes.  I can take it.
Brilliant post.  Is there any safe way to remove sda1 (labeled Dell Utility)?  It would be nice to reclaim that stolen space as well.

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-10-15
Here is another link about the Microsoft ram limit: [http://www.fudzilla.com/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=9851&Itemid=1](http://www.fudzilla.com/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=9851&Itemid=1)

If a computer has 2gb of ram, it is no longer a "netbook" by Microsoft's definition, and therefore the manufacturer can't sell it with XP netbook.

Dell obviously feels they cannot afford to lose the Mini 9 XP market, so they are complying with the MS restrictions. I'm sure it is only a matter of time before market pressures force the manufacturers to resist this limitation...

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-15
> **theacoustician said:**
> Brilliant post.  Is there any safe way to remove sda1 (labeled Dell Utility)?  It would be nice to reclaim that stolen space as well.

You can certainly delete the Dell Utility partition and create a new linux partition there if you like.  I think it's just diagnostic stuff.  I recommend you take a look at what is there just in case.  I haven't looked, myself.

However, if you want to merge that space with your existing root partition, you'll need to find some more powerful tools than the ones included in a base linux installation.  You'll need something that can *move* a partition.  I haven't had the need to do that for years, but the last time I did, I used Partition Magic.

---

### Post by theacoustician on 2008-10-15
> **trebonius said:**
> You can certainly delete the Dell Utility partition and create a new linux partition there if you like.  I think it's just diagnostic stuff.  I recommend you take a look at what is there just in case.  I haven't looked, myself.

However, if you want to merge that space with your existing root partition, you'll need to find some more powerful tools than the ones included in a base linux installation.  You'll need something that can *move* a partition.  I haven't had the need to do that for years, but the last time I did, I used Partition Magic.
Gparted?  I was kinda wondering why you didn't use it in your instructions.

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-15
> **BCurtisWX said:**
> What happens when you need to upgrade.. is it alright to upgrade with the ubuntu repository, or are things going to break?  How about when it comes time for 8.10, are we going to have to wait for dell to update things, or will we be able to upgrade fine 'n dandy?

Dell uses a custom set of repositories (you might notice in the source repositories list that the main repositories for Ubuntu are all disabled). So I would strongly recommend not to mix the two. besides, If you include them, you will get an architecture error.

As for 8.10, you will probably get an update when Dell decides so.

In general if you encounter a bug, just file in in launchpad as you would for your regular Ubuntu installation. Although Dell mini repos are machine specific, they are still hosted by Canonical, which is handling also updates.

---

---

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-15
> **gaussian said:**
> I have not ordered mine yet, not sure I will. But for me the big advantage of Dell was the Ubuntu pre-install compatible with Ubuntu repos. So I could install stuff that I need (e.g. Texlive, Emacs+Auctex, Tomboy, Tasque). Do the Dell repos contain everything there is in a standard Ubuntu repos or is it a subset?

As of now, I haven't encountered any missing package from the Ubuntu repos. All the packages you mention are available. The only missing repo is the backports. In any case, if something is missing or old, you can make backports yourself, the same way you do it in the regular Ubuntu. In this way, you create packages with the proper architecture (lpia). I made several backports, all working perfectly. I prefer this approach than to force the installation of a i386 package, since this doesn't show up on Synaptic.

There might be delays in updates of packages between the regular Ubuntu and the special dellmini, as it happened for firefox 3.0.3, updated only today. If you encounter packages that are not updated, file bugs in launchpad.

---

### Post by gaussian on 2008-10-15
> **feranick said:**
> As of now, I haven't encountered any missing package from the Ubuntu repos. All the packages you mention are available. The only missing repo is the backports. In any case, if something is missing or old, you can make backports yourself, the same way you do it in the regular Ubuntu. In this way, you create packages with the proper architecture (lpia). I made several backports, all working perfectly. I prefer this approach than to force the installation of a i386 package, since this doesn't show up on Synaptic.

There might be delays in updates of packages between the regular Ubuntu and the special dellmini, as it happened for firefox 3.0.3, updated only today. If you encounter packages that are not updated, file bugs in launchpad.

### Post by wolfkeeper on 2008-10-15
> **jonathankark said:**
> trebonius...

quick question..... I too was having problems with the initial partitioning of the mini 9. Following your instructions, I repartitioned, but something is off. I think I made the dell utilities bootable as well, so now, when i restart it goes straight to utilities, and I cant get into linux.

Unless you can get it from the restore CD you may need to create some sort of bootable USB drive with a disk partitioning tool to reset the boot flag.

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-15
> **jonathankark said:**
> trebonius...

quick question..... I too was having problems with the initial partitioning of the mini 9. Following your instructions, I repartitioned, but something is off. I think I made the dell utilities bootable as well, so now, when i restart it goes straight to utilities, and I cant get into linux.

If you have an external cdrom, boot with the Ubuntu cd and go into the live version.  You can use fdisk from there.  If not, there are lots of linux-based computer repair flash drive images you can download and use that will contain fdisk as well.  Let me know if you need help finding one.

And you are correct that the dell utility partition should not be marked as bootable.  Mine isn't.

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-15
> **wolfkeeper said:**
> The readme file included tells you where to put the files. I couldn't figure out how to put the three folders in /usr/share/skype (rights issue I'm guessing?)

After initial excitement with the static version, I had resolution problems with the webcam. Anyway here some instructions:

1. Download the static version (.tar.gz).
2. Uncompress anywhere on your home folder
3. Open the uncompressed skype folder
4. Double click on the file skype and select run.


However I found that the official packages actually works pretty well. To install it:

1. Download  the Ubuntu package
2. open terminal (menu Accessories)
3. move into the folder you downloaded the package
4. Type:

sudo apt-get -i --force-architecture skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb

---

### Post by wolfkeeper on 2008-10-16
You mean dpkg right? :)

---

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-16
> **wolfkeeper said:**
> You mean dpkg right? :)

Yes! revised instructions below. Sorry...

1. Download the Ubuntu package
2. open terminal (menu Accessories)
3. move into the folder you downloaded the package
4. Type:

```
sudo dpkg -i --force-architecture skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb
```

*) If you're confident that you got all of that done correctly, use the "w" command to write your changes to the hard drive.  Otherwise, use "q" to exit without making changes.

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-16
> **wolfkeeper said:**
> You probably typed "sudo apt-get" instead of "sudo dpkg"... feranick's instructions were incorrect but were corrected a couple of posts later. See post #370.

I am reposting here, with links:

1. Download [the Ubuntu package from Skype]("http://www.skype.com/go/getskype-linux-ubuntu")
2. open terminal (menu Accessories)
3. move into the folder you downloaded the package
4. Type:

```
sudo dpkg -i --force-architecture skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb
```

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-16
I enabled the ubuntu remix repository for the dell mini and downloaded several updates. I added the following to the software sources list (System->Administration->Software Source, Third-party software tab):

```
deb http://dell-mini.archive.canonical.com/ubuntu/ hardy-netbook-remix main universe multiverse restricted 
```

Among the updates, few bugs notable bugs were fixed. I list the most noticeable below (and I will keep adding them here).

1. VLC: the stock version has a bug in the full screen mode. The full screen mode, is not really full screen, it is limited the screen not covered by the panel bars. this is fixed with the additional repos.

### Post by gregconquest on 2008-10-16
My guess on this has to do with the EULA (End User Licensing Agreement) that Firefox *was* requiring. Several linux vendors shied away from using the Firefox brand name because they thought including an EULA went against the whole idea of open source. Firefox code could still be used, it being open, but the Firefox name could not. Firefox heard the complaints, re-examined the issue, and relaxed the rules.

[Ubuntu-Firefox EULA dustup reignites OSS licensing debate (arstechnica)]("http://arstechnica.com/news.ars/post/20080915-ubuntu-firefox-eula-dustup-reignites-oss-licensing-debate.html")

The iteration of Firefox that is being used on the mini 9 was apparently worked out during the time before Firefox relaxed the rules.

So, don't worry. Change the icon if you wish. It doesn't really matter that I can see.

Greg

---

### Post by snowpine on 2008-10-16

---

### Post by superm1 on 2008-10-16
> **snowpine said:**
> Can we confirm that in addition to having a customized user interface, the Dell Ubuntu also has a custom kernel?
A majority of the fixes that are in this kernel have already been pulled in as SRU's to the standard hardy kernel or into the intrepid kernel ( but not all of them, bluetooth for example on some platforms, not yet).

You can see the kernel (and it's kernel config) here if you would like:

[http://kernel.ubuntu.com/git?p=mid-team/hardy-netbook.git;a=summary](http://kernel.ubuntu.com/git?p=mid-team/hardy-netbook.git;a=summary)

### Post by Rallg on 2008-10-16
My mini 9 came with XP. When Ubuntu settles down in 8.10, I'll dual boot it.

Using Xubuntu 8.04.1, booted from USB, I noticed that the screen shows at what I suppose must be VGA resolution. Certainly it is not optimised for the 1024x600 screen, or even 1024x768. The hardware menu doesn't offer me an alternative.

The same Xubuntu works fine on my Vostro 1000, where I normally use (non-X)Ubuntu 8.04.1.

So it looks like 8.10 might be necessary, perhaps the special verison for this kind of device, as mentioned elsewhere.

Otherwise, so far I like the mini 9, with the glaring exception of the quote key placement. A few keys (tilde, braces, backslash, etc.) are accessed via the Fn key. That could be a problem if you are using something such as a JavaScript that requires you to click while holding down one of those particular keys.

I can type reasonably quickly on the mini 9, except for the quotes. Be sure to turn off touchpad tapping, or you'll go crazy.

My device has Bluetooth but no camera. I haven't tried the Bluetooth yet. WiFi works as expected.

[LIST]
[*]Multiple applications have been modified to run better on the mini 9
[LIST]
[*]For the small screen size
[*]Usability.  Example, compare the bluetooth experience on the mini 9 install with the standard 8.04.1 install, or the network manager experience.
[*]Large bugs
[/LIST]
[*]All updates that normally come in normally as Stable Release Updates (SRUs) will be time delayed until they are verified to not break anything on the mini 9.
[/LIST]

### Post by redDEADresolve on 2008-10-17
It recognizes that usb entry as a cd rom from the installation.

I writing a guide on [www.ubuntumini.com](www.ubuntumini.com) right now for it. It just involves fixing you fstab.

Here's the fix, [http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/fixing-usb-mounting-problems-after.html](http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/fixing-usb-mounting-problems-after.html).
___

Second I really love ume-launcher. I removed any and all packages I wouldn't use, including Compiz. The Firefox glich seems to be a with ume-launcher. After removing all compiz packages it still happens. but doesn't when launching Firefox in gnome normally.

### Post by pHreaksYcle on 2008-10-17
> What exactly are the advantages of doing this? Seems to counter the idea that Dell actually did some work on customization, as indicated earlier in this thread like here:

From redDEAD's comment on UbuntuMini.com:
> First it takes care of the hard drive problem. The Mini OS version on Ubuntu that comes installed on the laptop only uses four gigs leaving any other hard drive space unusable. There are workarounds but its not the same as properly setting things up. By separating your system and home partitions you make updating distributions faster, safer and easier. You don't run the risk of losing data if your operating system gets messed up. Netbook Remix is much faster then Dell's custom UI. Ubuntu 8.10 (as opposed to 8.04) offers significant updates to the Linux kernel, the network manager and includes the ability to use tabs in Nautilus. These three are enough to make me want to upgrade. I will be traveling from state to state with this laptop, I want the network manager to work.

---

### Post by pbags on 2008-10-17
> **feranick said:**
> I am reposting here, with links:

1. Download [the Ubuntu package from Skype]("http://www.skype.com/go/getskype-linux-ubuntu")
2. open terminal (menu Accessories)
3. move into the folder you downloaded the package
4. Type:

```
sudo dpkg -i --force-architecture skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb
```

Eureka! That worked. Though I got this output when I ran that command:
peter@peter:~/Desktop/Downloads$ sudo dpkg -i --force-architecture skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb
dpkg - warning, overriding problem because --force enabled:
 package architecture (i386) does not match system (lpia)
Selecting previously deselected package skype.
(Reading database ... 96471 files and directories currently installed.)
Unpacking skype (from skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_i386.deb) ...
Setting up skype (2.0.0.72-1) ...
peter@peter:~/Desktop/Downloads$ 

What does that ominous sounding warning mean?

### Post by mfm3 on 2008-10-17
> First it takes care of the hard drive problem. The Mini OS version on Ubuntu that comes installed on the laptop only uses four gigs leaving any other hard drive space unusable. There are workarounds but its not the same as properly setting things up. By separating your system and home partitions you make updating distributions faster, safer and easier. You don't run the risk of losing data if your operating system gets messed up.:

This was very easy to do with the factory install: use gparted to format the space, copy contents of home folder to new partition, edit fstab to mount new partition to /home, reboot. Took about 10 minutes after I pulled it out of the box.

> Netbook Remix is much faster then Dell's custom UI.

Not according to other reports....

> Ubuntu 8.10 (as opposed to 8.04) offers significant updates to the Linux kernel, the network manager and includes the ability to use tabs in Nautilus.

### Post by yelsn on 2008-10-17
> **pbags said:**
> ...
dpkg - warning, overriding problem because --force enabled:
 package architecture (i386) does not match system (lpia)
...

What does that ominous sounding warning mean?

This just means that you're forcing the OS to install a package with a different architecture than the rest of your OS.
In this case, it shouldn't be a big deal since lpia is basically i386 with some customizations.  Your processor should be able to mix i386 programs/libraries with lpia programs/libraries just fine.

---

### Post by racedog on 2008-10-17
I too got a solid state drive on my Mini 9 with the partition set smaller than the 16 GB capacity of my SSD.  

Someone had previously given out directions for enlarging the size of a partition using the command line.

That will certainly work, but if the command line intimidates you, you can always try booting into Ubuntu from some kind of USB media like a CD ROM drive, hard drive or even a memory stick (see [pendrivelinux.com]("http://www.pendrivelinux.com")).  With that you can resize the partition using the graphical partition editor.  

Of course, you have to have a USB storage device handy.  Just another option.  Something like that's good to do backup with too.

Love my mini 9!  Ubuntu rocks!

### Post by gregconquest on 2008-10-17
> **nanog said:**
> Another reason *not to* re-install ubuntu are the fluendo gstreamer codecs that allow me, for the first time, to play windows media streams at every freaking site (even Bloomberg!). A reinstall will delete these.

This is one issue that I've only seen questions on and no answers yet. On the very useful blog ["Ubuntu on the Dell Inspiron Mini 9 - Guides, Tweaks & Hacks for Your Dell Mini 9" [link]]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/"), we have this entry:

["Ubuntu 8.10 Intrepid Ibex on the Dell Mini 9" [link]]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/installing-ubuntu-netbook-remix.html")
> Media Codecs
Ubuntu will ask if you want to install the proper codec when you try to open an unsupported file. Most codecs you will try to play can be installed this way. If you like The most a guide for the all major codecs can be found ["here" [link]]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/reinstallng-codecs.html") and encrypted DVD playback and w32codecs ["here" [link]]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/medibuntu-multimedia-entertainment.html").

This is wonderful, but there are still a few codecs that are included on the DELL-customized ubuntu that aren't installable otherwise, it seems.  I can't find it now, but **Real Media / Real Player codecs** are included in addition to the ones nanog mentions above.

So, **is there any way for us to get the extra codecs out of a DELL ubuntu install and migrate them to a stock ubuntu install**? I'll have both the license to these codecs, and I'll the codecs themselves on my mini 9 before I wipe the SSD and install ubuntu Itrepid.

Greg

### Post by cyclist23 on 2008-10-18
I received my Dell mini today.  Ordered it on October 8, estimated ship date was November 13, Dell shipped it October 15, and DHL delivered it to my house today, the 17.

My config is white, Ubuntu, 8GB SSD, 2GB RAM, Bluetooth, and a 1.3MP webcam.

I've seen every photo and video of the Mini on the internet, but still would not have imagined how small it is in person.  It is absolutely gorgeous.  Feels excellent in my hand.  Has an amazing screen with no dead pixel.  The keyboard is very typeable with a few minor flaws and one major one.

1. It is clicky.  The machine is dead silent, except for the keyboard.
2. I keep hitting the enter key when I want to do contraction.
3. I hit the apostrophe key everytime I want to use the left directional key.
4. The bump on F and J should be more prominent and centered.  I have a hard time feeling it because of the closeness to the CV and NM keys.

Now the major one, and the reason I'm sending it back.
5. The spacebar is unuseable to me.  The bottom of the spacebar is stiff, it feels like it is stuck to the trackpad.  The left edge is also raised.

The trackpad is also a bother.

1. I keep accidentally hitting it while typing.
2. It is very sensitive, even after adjusting it.
3. The vertical scroll zone is too large and I accidentally scroll when I don't want to.
4. Scrolling is hard to control.  It scrolls more line than I wanted to.

Except for the keyboard and trackpad, the build quality of the Mini is excellent.

This is my first time on Ubuntu and I'm loving it.  I uninstall yahoo toolbar and updated firefox to 3.0.3.  Maximus auto maximizing is a nice feature.  My 8GB SSD show up as 6.8GB with 5.2GB used and 1.6GB free.  2G of RAM is showing up as 883.2MB.  Bluetooth, wifi, webcam, and mic are working perfectly out of the box.

### Post by Rallg on 2008-10-18
About the touchpad: If you have it set so that you can "click" by tapping, then you are in for hard times (whether Ubuntu or XP). Due to the closeness of everything, it's hard to avoid the touchpad sometimes.

### Post by nrdlevans on 2008-10-18
I can report that Dell is actually shipping the 910.  I am typing on one.  I ordered it with Ubuntu, and all the bells and whistles.  I initially forgot to order the DVD drive however Dell was happy to take my money on one.

So far I have found two things wrong with it.  

1) The 16GB drive came formatted as a 4GB os partion and 12 GB unpartition space.  I used the Dell provided restore disk and it reformatted the entire drive into a single parition.  An annoyance, not a huge problem.  The restore completely restored the system.

2) Virtual box does not work!! It reorts my system as a "LPIA" and not a 386 based architecture so it refuses to install.  I have not found a solution yet.

Out of the box my bluetooth box was found and began working.  I can disable the touchpad so I am happy.  The wireless and integrated ethernet works.  I am finding the wireless connectin a bit flakey however I do not think it is Dell but my networks fault.

### Post by gregconquest on 2008-10-18
> **nrdlevans said:**
>  . . . 
1) The 16GB drive came formatted as a 4GB os partion and 12 GB unpartition space.  I used the Dell provided restore disk and it reformatted the entire drive into a single parition.  An annoyance, not a huge problem.  The restore completely restored the system.

"Completely restored"? Back to a 4GB OS partition and 12GB unused? If so, the restore "breaks" it.

> I just ordered a 2GB memory stick to max out the memory of the box.

You might want to read a few more of the posts here. Though who wants to read 45 pages? Anyway, there have been a couple of posts saying that **DELL's kernel does not address over 1GB of RAM**. The BIOS may show it, but the OS doesn't use it. I don't think this has been confirmed, and no one is sure if OS updates will fix this. It seems intentional, though.

Also, the most exciting thing here is the compatibility of ubuntu 8.10, coming out in final form October 30th. Everything just works . . . Well, almost everything. You need to change one line of a script for the audio to work, and the codecs for proprietary audio and video needs to be installed.

Check this out:
[http://www.ubuntumini.com/]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/")

Greg

### Post by nrdlevans on 2008-10-18
No, the restore did not restore the system to the 4GB format.  It simply created one partition.  I suspect Dells problems boil down to an operator loading he drives with the wrong image.  Having worked in a computer manufacturing process (not Dell), I could understand the problem.  Operator "x" loads all 1000 drives with the same image not realizing that 40% of the drives were larger than 4GB.

I have seen the references to 8.10.  It looks real interesting.  If it does not report the OS as "LPIA" then some installs might start working.  (That comes from UNAME -A"

### Post by Talon2 on 2008-10-18
> **anaxolotl said:**
> 
My config: 512 Ram, 16gb ssd, 1.3 mp webcam and bluetooth.

Anyone see something there that would have cause delays while everyone else is getting theirs?  maybe they are out of 512 chips.



My config:  1gb ram, 8gb ssd, 1.3 webcam, and bluetooth.

Ordered it on 9/4 and got it last week.

My thoughts:

- The more I use it the more I like it.  After using a regular size Thinkpad for years it does take some getting used to.

- The boot is so quick that you have to hit the "2" to enter bios before the bios intial screen even becomes visible or you will miss it.  Not a big problem but you do have to get used to it.

- My 8gb ssd has 4gb of unused space.  It is no big deal for me as I installed a 4 gb hcsd card which works great.  I'll work on this when Dell announces a fix.

- I did switch from the Dell interface to the regular Ubuntu interface.  It is very easy to do and you can switch back when you want.  The Dell interface has some good ideas but I'm very used to the normal Ubuntu interface.

- I turned the speakers off as I want the system quiet unless I am wearing headphones.  This is easy to do.

- I turned the touchpad tap off to avoid unwanted clicks.  This is also very easy to do.

- I tested all hardware except for bluetooth and everything "just works."  I can't test bluetooth as I have no bluetooth devices.  Wireless networking works very well.  Battery life is good.  I haven't timed battery life but I'll estimate a full charge to last between 3-4 hours.

---

### Post by nanog on 2008-10-19
[COLOR="Red"][SIZE="3"]Warning: contrary to what was mentioned earlier the fluendo codecs ARE NOT in the medibuntu repos! IF YOU REINSTALL YOU WILL LOSE THESE PROPRIETARY CODECS.[/SIZE][/COLOR]

gstreamer0.10-fluendo-plugins
Windows Media and MP3 codec support for gstreamer

The codecs that dell included can be purchased at canonical here:
[http://shop.canonical.com/product_info.php?products_id=242&osCsid=f862569f7330fddae34a6372c91fa430](http://shop.canonical.com/product_info.php?products_id=242&osCsid=f862569f7330fddae34a6372c91fa430)

I can now play video at every site that was non-functional with restricted extras/medibuntu codecs. I was so impressed that I bought the codecs for my other laptops. :)

### Post by 2damncommon on 2008-10-19
> **gregconquest said:**
> Anyway, there have been a couple of posts saying that **DELL's kernel does not address over 1GB of RAM**. The BIOS may show it, but the OS doesn't use it. I don't think this has been confirmed, and no one is sure if OS updates will fix this. It seems intentional, though.
I can confirm that I installed a 2GB RAM stick before I ever even booted the system. I checked the BIOS and it confirmed 2GB. Even 
"sudo lshw-gtk" (once installed) shows 2GB of memory. However "free -m" shows a different story.

After some googling it seems to me this is an issue with the default Ubuntu lpia kernel not Dell. Since Dell is only going to offer their laptop with 1GB of RAM because of limitations of the Windows XP licensing they have no reason to be concerned with the kernel offering up to 4GB of RAM any more than they are concerned with the kernel offering 32GB of RAM.

I just spent the evening attempting to recompile the kernel. I made one change of adding high memory support. kpkg fails with a bug/error. My "make" bzImage fails at providing the correct graphics. I in no way claim to be an expert kernel compiler. If I don't see better advice I'll try again.

I have fixed the 4GB partition, enabled the Compiz spinning cube, and continue to add anything I want. The default "sugar-emulator" seems to be lacking. How can the mini play with an olpc?

### Post by pHreaksYcle on 2008-10-19
[IMG]http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3165/2950569978_631b5eaee7.jpg[/IMG]

Okay, for those that didn't read the rest of the posts (not that I blame you, but at least search it):

[Dell repos]("http://dell-mini.archive.canonical.com/ubuntu/") have all the files that you would get through updates.
[www.ubuntumini.com]("http://www.ubuntumini.com") including how to install Ubuntu Netbook Remix and how well it works.

**A (very) condensed version of the site is:**
1. [How to install Intrepid with the netbook remix packages]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/installing-ubuntu-on-dell-inspiron-mini.html") (**spoiler: it's fricking awesome!**)
2. [How to install the codecs]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/reinstallng-codecs.html")     [more codecs]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/medibuntu-multimedia-entertainment.html")- because people outside the US do not have to pay for these codecs, they are available for free. Please do not use these in regions that prohibit it. Also, [B]please report what functions these packages do not enable, I am interested to know.
[/B]3. [Overview]("http://www.ubuntumini.com/2008/10/ubuntu-810-intrepid-ibex-on-dell-mini-9.html")

If you found the site helpful, drop a comment, we've had tons of views and almost no comments.

Hope this helps!
pHreak

### Post by nanog on 2008-10-19
phreakcycle, the fluendo codecs allow me to play bloomberg videos.  [COLOR="Red"]**these streams do not work with the mplayer codecs available at medibuntu. i can provide many more examples. medibuntu gstreamer wm codecs have been very broken for some time.**[/COLOR]

"screw buying it again"
you can and will do whatever you like. i personally prefer to have streaming video that just works.

i have hardy working just fine with **[COLOR="Red"]network manager 7[/COLOR]** and the dust theme from the intrepid community themes pack. i personally see ZERO advantage to removing the fluendo codecs and installing 8.10. why the heck would anyone want to spend hours reinstalling ubuntu when dell has already done it for you -- and with an optimized kernel to boot.


```

~$ uname -a
Linux  2.6.24-19-lpia #1 SMP Tue Jul 29 14:02:05 UTC 2008 i686 GNU/Linux

~$ dpkg -l | grep network-manager
ii  network-manager                            0.7~~svn20081008t224042-0ubuntu3~nm1~hardy2          network management framework daemon
ii  network-manager-gnome                      0.7~~svn20081012t133407-0ubuntu1~nm1~hardy1          network management framework (GNOME frontend
```

screenshot of hardy running with the kin theme attached.

Repos for nm 0.7svn and community themes (you need to install the community themes package):

```
#community themes ppa
deb http://ppa.launchpad.net/kwwii/ubuntu hardy main
deb-src http://ppa.launchpad.net/kwwii/ubuntu hardy main

#network manager ppa
deb http://ppa.launchpad.net/network-manager/ubuntu hardy main
deb-src http://ppa.launchpad.net/network-manager/ubuntu hardy main

```
[B]disclaimer: use of 3rd party repos could damage your system.
[/B]

magicfab, thanks for the helpful post.
the dvd player is available from canonical and  only if you demonstrate a valid license. there is no evidence that dell or canonical will suppply the fluendo codecs. until then i personally am not upgrading to intrepid.

### Post by nanog on 2008-10-20
[QUOTE=RichTJ99;6001441]I thought that there were potential issues with using a "regular" Ubuntu install vs the "custom" dell one.  

Are there pros & cons to each install?

There have been posts complaining about power management, suspend, and wireless following custom installs. YMMV.

The dell version has an apparently optimized kernel and modifications/codecs that improve multimedia experience. I personally find that streaming vidio/audio works flawlessly even at multiple sites that are non-functional in default installs. There seems to be customization of flash as well because it is normally fairly buggy in 8.04.1.

### Post by 2damncommon on 2008-10-21
> **feranick said:**
> Filed bug:

[https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+bug/286258](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+bug/286258)
As I said in a previous post, it appears the default lpia kernel does not add support for large memory. Not sure that is really a "bug".

I had problems attempting to compile a new kernel with only the change to add large memory support.

Following a Ubuntu kernel compiling guide I use the command:
"make-kpkg kernel_image kernel_headers" and receive the error:
"exec debian/rules  DEBIAN_REVISION=..-10.00.Custom  kernel_image kernel_headers 
debian/ruleset/misc/checks.mk:36: *** Error. I do not know where the kernel image goes to [kimagedest undefined] The usual case for this is that I could not determine which arch or subarch this machine belongs to. Please specify a subarch, and try again..  Stop."

"dpkg-architecture" displays:
"DEB_BUILD_ARCH=lpia
DEB_BUILD_ARCH_OS=linux
DEB_BUILD_ARCH_CPU=i686
DEB_BUILD_GNU_CPU=i686
DEB_BUILD_GNU_SYSTEM=linux-gnulp
DEB_BUILD_GNU_TYPE=i686-linux-gnulp
DEB_HOST_ARCH=lpia
DEB_HOST_ARCH_OS=linux
DEB_HOST_ARCH_CPU=i686
DEB_HOST_GNU_CPU=i686
DEB_HOST_GNU_SYSTEM=linux-gnulp
DEB_HOST_GNU_TYPE=i686-linux-gnulp"

Another problem was that the "kernel-source" package seemed to install 2.6.24.3 sources. I had to download "linux-source-2.6.24_2.6.24-19.36_all.deb" manually.

TIA for any advice. Probably a small fix to be able to run the compile?

---

### Post by superm1 on 2008-10-21
> **nanog said:**
> [quote=RichTJ99;6001441]I thought that there were potential issues with using a "regular" Ubuntu install vs the "custom" dell one.  

Are there pros & cons to each install?

There have been posts complaining about power management, suspend, and wireless following custom installs. YMMV.

The dell version has an apparently optimized kernel and modifications/codecs that improve multimedia experience. I personally find that streaming vidio/audio works flawlessly even at multiple sites that are non-functional in default installs. There seems to be customization of flash as well because it is normally fairly buggy in 8.04.1.
Something else that hasn't been mentioned, the boot process for the Mini OS image will be significantly quicker than for a standard Ubuntu install.  There are a lot of factors that play into those customizations.

On that note, I need to install wicd because of a weird password thing with Network Manager and WPA passwords.[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=787098#2](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=787098#2) (password gets scrambled and/or extended after every reboot) is my problem, and [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=787098#15](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=787098#15) (install wicd) should be the solution, but I'm not sure if it's a good idea to enable the necessary sources and get wicd because of the lpia/386 and fancy dell repositories thing discussed earlier (default hardy install). ### Post by 2damncommon on 2008-10-21
> **nrdlevans said:**
> Two gigabytes is considered large amounts of memory?  I will admit my first computer had the huge amount of memory of 16k however those days are **_long _**long gone.
When compiling a Linux kernel, the choices under "High Memory Support" are "off", "4GB", and "64GB". The option chosen on the default lpia kernel is "off". Apparently the "4GB" choice is needed to detect and use 2GB of RAM.

### Post by feranick on 2008-10-23
> **RichTJ99 said:**
> I have to say, I am starting to have second thoughts about my Mini (that wont ship for at least two more weeks).  I am wondering if the 386 vs LPIA will be a large (excuse this) PIA.  

I have a Dell XPS 1210 (12" laptop) running Ubuntu & I love it.  I think the mini would be a better travel laptop (something I keep in the car or plane).  

I am concerned about not being able to use regular .deb files with the 'stock' version of Ubuntu from dell vs reinstalling it from scratch.

My biggest concern would be using the regular 8.04/8.10 & having the suspend work properly.  I would prefer to use the 386 & maybe lose a little speed (not 30% speed but the 5-10% gain I suspect the 'custom dell' rom used).

Thanks,
Rich

Really the i386 deb is a non issue. You can easily repackage any deb outside the repos into an lpia one. And really there aren't many. The reason you want lpia packages instead of forcing the i386, is that while the former will show up as an installed package on Synaptics, the later won't, so good luck uninstalling it. For example here's the directions to convert the stock i386 package for Skype into an lpia package:

1. Download the Ubuntu version of Skype
2. Use the Archive manager to uncompress it. Alternatively, right click on the deb package and select "extract here"
3. Rename the uncompressed folder, by changing the ending from i386 to lpia. (the folder should be now named: skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia)
4. Open the folder
5. There are 3 files. remove the debian-binary file
6. Decompress control.tar.gz. There are tree files in it.
7. Make a folder "DEBIAN" in the main folder, and placed the 3 files you just decompressed inside it.
8. Open one of the files inside DEBIAN, "control". Change the line "Architecture: i386" into "Architecture: lpia"
9. Open the other archive data.tar.gz. There is a folder "." inside. Open it (double click). Select both the folders (usr and etc) and select uncompress.
10. Remove the files control.tar.gz and data.tar.gz
11. You should now have your main folder with the following inside:
DEBIAN folder with tree text files in it (conffiles, control, md5sums); a "usr" folder and a "etc" folder.
12. You are now ready to prepare your lpia deb folder.
13. From the command line, go to the folder that contains your main skype folder you just prepared (most likely named: skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia).
14. type: dpkg --build skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia
15. Your deb package is ready. Double click on it and follow instruction. After installation it should appear in Synaptic, and from there you can remove it at any time.
16. Enjoy!!!


Besides, I made a bunch of backports from standard Ubuntu repositories, all working great in my mini once packaged as lpia.

### Post by mfm3 on 2008-10-24
xorg.conf:

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Generic Keyboard"
	Driver		"kbd"
	Option		"XkbRules"	"xorg"
	Option		"XkbModel"	"pc105"
	Option		"XkbLayout"	"us"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Configured Mouse"
	Driver		"mouse"
	Option		"CorePointer"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Synaptics Touchpad"
	Driver		"synaptics"
	Option		"SendCoreEvents"	"true"
	Option		"Device"		"/dev/psaux"
	Option		"Protocol"		"auto-dev"
	Option		"SHMConfig"		"true"
EndSection

Section "Device"
	Identifier	"Configured Video Device"
	Option		"UseFBDev"		"true"
EndSection

Section "Monitor"
	Identifier	"Configured Monitor"
EndSection

Section "Screen"
	Identifier	"Default Screen"
	Monitor		"Configured Monitor"
	Device		"Configured Video Device"
EndSection

Section "ServerLayout"
	Identifier	"Default Layout"
	Screen		"Default Screen"
	InputDevice	"Synaptics Touchpad"
EndSection

Section "DRI"
	Mode 0666
EndSection

---

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-24
> **feranick said:**
> I have a problem with my xorg.conf file. I basically manually downgraded from Ubuntu remix (dell-mini repository), to the original configuration, but I cannot go past safe-mode. 

Would someone please post their xorg.conf file from the stock mini?

Many thanks!

# xorg.conf (X.Org X Window System server configuration file)
#
# This file was generated by dexconf, the Debian X Configuration tool, using
# values from the debconf database.
#
# Edit this file with caution, and see the xorg.conf manual page.
# (Type "man xorg.conf" at the shell prompt.)
#
# This file is automatically updated on xserver-xorg package upgrades *only*
# if it has not been modified since the last upgrade of the xserver-xorg
# package.
#
# If you have edited this file but would like it to be automatically updated
# again, run the following command:
#   sudo dpkg-reconfigure -phigh xserver-xorg

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Generic Keyboard"
	Driver		"kbd"
	Option		"XkbRules"	"xorg"
	Option		"XkbModel"	"pc105"
	Option		"XkbLayout"	"us"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Configured Mouse"
	Driver		"mouse"
	Option		"CorePointer"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Synaptics Touchpad"
	Driver		"synaptics"
	Option		"SendCoreEvents"	"true"
	Option		"Device"		"/dev/psaux"
	Option		"Protocol"		"auto-dev"
	Option		"SHMConfig"		"true"
EndSection

Section "Device"
	Identifier	"Configured Video Device"
	Option		"UseFBDev"		"true"
EndSection

Section "Monitor"
	Identifier	"Configured Monitor"
EndSection

Section "Screen"
	Identifier	"Default Screen"
	Monitor		"Configured Monitor"
	Device		"Configured Video Device"
EndSection

Section "ServerLayout"
	Identifier	"Default Layout"
	Screen		"Default Screen"
	InputDevice	"Synaptics Touchpad"
EndSection

Section "DRI"
	Mode 0666
EndSection

### Post by trebonius on 2008-10-24
Hey, guys.  Some of you have complained about accidentally causing clicks, taps and scrolling on the touchpad when typing.  Try this command:
syndaemon -dtK

I put an auto-start in my sessions settings.  It just disables tapping and scrolling with the touchpad for two seconds after each key pressed on the keyboard.  The -t flag disables only tapping and scrolling (not movement) and the -K flag ignores modifier keys and shortcuts.

---

### Post by yakker.yak on 2008-10-24
> **trebonius said:**
> Hey, guys.  Some of you have complained about accidentally causing clicks, taps and scrolling on the touchpad when typing.  Try this command:
syndaemon -dtK

I put an auto-start in my sessions settings.  It just disables tapping and scrolling with the touchpad for two seconds after each key pressed on the keyboard.  The -t flag disables only tapping and scrolling (not movement) and the -K flag ignores modifier keys and shortcuts.

You can also permanently disable tap-clicking by adding the following to your /etc/X11/xorg.conf:

Option          "TapButton1"            "0"

i.e.

Section "InputDevice"
  Identifier      "Synaptics Touchpad"
  etc. etc. etc.
  Option          "TapButton1"            "0"
EndSection

You'll need to restart the X server, reboot, etc. for the change to take effect.

---

### Post by bd_thompson on 2008-10-24
> **yakker.yak said:**
> You can also permanently disable tap-clicking by adding the following to your /etc/X11/xorg.conf:

Option          "TapButton1"            "0"

i.e.

Section "InputDevice"
  Identifier      "Synaptics Touchpad"
  etc. etc. etc.
  Option          "TapButton1"            "0"
EndSection

You'll need to restart the X server, reboot, etc. for the change to take effect.

You can also use the menu system.

SYSTEM > PREFERENCES > MOUSE

---

### Post by psignosis on 2008-10-25
> **trebonius said:**
> Hey, guys.  Some of you have complained about accidentally causing clicks, taps and scrolling on the touchpad when typing.  Try this command:
syndaemon -dtK

I put an auto-start in my sessions settings.  It just disables tapping and scrolling with the touchpad for two seconds after each key pressed on the keyboard.  The -t flag disables only tapping and scrolling (not movement) and the -K flag ignores modifier keys and shortcuts.

Or you could change the user preferences in Gnome:

System > Preferences > Mouse > Touchpad ...
uncheck "Enable Mouse clicks with touchpad" and/or
uncheck "Enable Vertical Scrolling" and/or uncheck "Enable Horizontal Scrolling"

### Post by Rallg on 2008-10-27
1. I used UNETBOOTIN (a Windows program) to put live Ubuntu on USB (having downloaded the ISO separately). Then, I booted to the USB, and used it to install Ubuntu to the main drive. Get UNETBOOTIN (search for it), and its use is fairly self-explanatory.

2. For those who have the XP version and are adding Ubuntu: The XP version comes with a utility program called "Wiss" that allows to you easily turn WiFi and/or Bluetooth on or off, to save power. I discovered that if WiFi is turned off while in XP, then the computer is later booted to Ubuntu (even much later), the WiFi is still off. So, if you are dual booting, before complaining that your WiFi doesn't work, check to see if you turned it off on the XP side. (I haven't checked what Bluetooth does.)

---

### Post by sd73ta on 2008-10-27
> **feranick said:**
> Really the i386 deb is a non issue. You can easily repackage any deb outside the repos into an lpia one. And really there aren't many. The reason you want lpia packages instead of forcing the i386, is that while the former will show up as an installed package on Synaptics, the later won't, so good luck uninstalling it. For example here's the directions to convert the stock i386 package for Skype into an lpia package:

1. Download the Ubuntu version of Skype
2. Use the Archive manager to uncompress it. Alternatively, right click on the deb package and select "extract here"
3. Rename the uncompressed folder, by changing the ending from i386 to lpia. (the folder should be now named: skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia)
4. Open the folder
5. There are 3 files. remove the debian-binary file
6. Decompress control.tar.gz. There are tree files in it.
7. Make a folder "DEBIAN" in the main folder, and placed the 3 files you just decompressed inside it.
8. Open one of the files inside DEBIAN, "control". Change the line "Architecture: i386" into "Architecture: lpia"
9. Open the other archive data.tar.gz. There is a folder "." inside. Open it (double click). Select both the folders (usr and etc) and select uncompress.
10. Remove the files control.tar.gz and data.tar.gz
11. You should now have your main folder with the following inside:
DEBIAN folder with tree text files in it (conffiles, control, md5sums); a "usr" folder and a "etc" folder.
12. You are now ready to prepare your lpia deb folder.
13. From the command line, go to the folder that contains your main skype folder you just prepared (most likely named: skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia).
14. type: dpkg --build skype-debian_2.0.0.72-1_lpia
15. Your deb package is ready. Double click on it and follow instruction. After installation it should appear in Synaptic, and from there you can remove it at any time.
16. Enjoy!!!


Besides, I made a bunch of backports from standard Ubuntu repositories, all working great in my mini once packaged as lpia.

Ive tried the above with opera and it installed and there is an icon in the internet pulldown but it doesnt launch when i click on it. any suggestions
